{"uri": "8256967790", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-03", "time": "05:37:54", "dateTime": "2024-08-03T05:37:54Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-03T05:37:14Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9764705896377563, "url": "https://www.myjoyonline.com/kamala-harris-formally-chosen-as-democratic-nominee/", "title": "Kamala Harris formally chosen as Democratic nominee - MyJoyOnline", "body": "US Vice-President Kamala Harris has passed the threshold to clinch the Democratic presidential nomination in a vote of party delegates.\n\nUS Vice-President Kamala Harris has passed the threshold to clinch the Democratic presidential nomination in a vote of party delegates.\n\nSpeaking by telephone, Ms Harris said she was \"honoured to be the presumptive nominee\" as the virtual roll call continues ahead of the Democratic National Convention (DNC) in Chicago later this month.\n\nMs Harris is the first black woman and first South Asian woman to become the White House standard-bearer for a major US political party.\n\nIf she defeats Donald Trump, the Republican nominee, in November she would be America's first female president.\n\nShe ran unopposed in the virtual roll call after President Joe Biden stepped aside last month and quickly endorsed her. Several potential rivals followed his lead.\n\nOn Friday afternoon, Ms Harris formally became the nominee after securing the support of 2,350 delegates, the threshold required to earn the nomination.\n\n\"We believe in the promise of America and that's what this campaign is about,\" she said in brief remarks by phone as she crossed the benchmark.\n\n\"We are in this, we are on the road and it's not going to be easy, but we're going to get this done.\"\n\nIn total, Democrats have said 3,923 delegates - or 99% of the participants - plan to vote for her.\n\nThe rollcall began on Thursday and ends on Monday.\n\nPresidential and vice-presidential nominees are typically anointed at their party conventions, but the relatively late date of the 2024 DNC risks falling afoul of state ballot access laws.\n\nMs Harris, 59, was born in Oakland, California, and is the first Democratic nominee in the party's nearly 200-year history to hail from a western state.\n\nShe rose through the ranks of state politics from San Francisco district attorney to California attorney general and then US senator.\n\nBefore dropping out of the race, Mr Biden had easily won the Democratic primary. He did not face stiff opposition despite voter concerns about his age and had won backing from 99% of pledged DNC delegates.\n\nBut the 81-year-old faced escalating pressure from within the party to withdraw after a poor June debate performance against Trump.\n\nThe decision to hold a virtual nominating process ahead of the 19-22 August convention was made while Mr Biden was still the presumptive candidate.\n\nIt came in response to rules for ballot access in the state of Ohio, which requires that candidates for the November ballot be formally selected 90 days before the election - or by 7 August.\n\nRepublican leaders in the state had warned they would enforce the law and, though lawmakers eventually created an exemption as they have done in the past, Democrats said an early rollcall would pre-empt further risks of their candidates being excluded from the ballot.\n\nDelegates do not need to vote on the vice-presidential pick.\n\nMs Harris is expected to name her running mate by Monday.\n\nThe Trump campaign and some Republicans have criticised the replacement of Mr Biden with Ms Harris, arguing she is the first major party candidate to secure the nomination without holding a press conference or a sit-down interview. Some have referred to the substitution as a \"coup\".\n\nBut Ms Harris has hit the campaign trail hard since Mr Biden's endorsement, making the case against Trump in multiple campaign rallies and fundraisers across battleground states.\n\nOn Friday, the campaign announced it had raised more than $310m (\u00a3242m) in the month of July, with more than two-thirds of people donating money for the first time.\n\nThat figure is more than double the $138m raised by the Trump campaign last month and marks the biggest haul of the 2024 election cycle so far.", "source": {"uri": "myjoyonline.com", Some have referred to the substitution as a \"coup\".\n\nBut Ms Harris has hit the campaign trail hard since Biden's endorsement, making the case against Trump in multiple campaign rallies and {"uri": "8261846062", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "13:03:53", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T13:03:53Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T13:02:53Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8588235378265381, "url": "https://www.nwitimes.com/harris-wins-democratic-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_da8509bb-b272-59b0-8bd3-d8c27e262ef1.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "By ROBERT YOON - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nKamala Harris is now Democratic presidential nominee, will face off against Donald Trump this fall By SEUNG MIN KIM - Associated Press\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. {"uri": "2024-08-445749889", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "13:15:42", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T13:15:42Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T12:30:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8392156958580017, "url": "https://omaha.com/harris-wins-democratic-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_4abe660d-0b45-5352-86d7-d16593e18974.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "Kamala Harris was set to announce her running mate Tuesday before the pair head out on a five-day swing through America's hottest battleground states, just three months out from the presidential election. Who are the contenders? Answer with FRANCE 24's international affairs commentator Dougl...\n\nWASHINGTON -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nKamala Harris is now Democratic presidential nominee, will face off against Donald Trump this fall\n\nBy SEUNG MIN KIM - Associated Press\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years", "source": {"uri": "omaha.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Omaha.com"}, "authors": [{"uri": {"uri": "2024-08-445578112", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "10:48:53", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T10:48:53Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T10:25:54Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8196078538894653, "url": "https://www.newsmax.com/politics/kamala-harris-democratic-national-convention-roll-call/2024/08/06/id/1175388/", "title": "Harris Wins Democrat Presidential Nomination in Virtual Roll Call", "body": "Democrat delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee.\n\nHarris claimed the nomination after a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which the DNC said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET Monday.\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices, or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democrat governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nNow that Harris is the Democrats' presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democrat nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democrat delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \"a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature eventually made the change at the behest of Gov. Mike DeWine, also a Republican, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as planned.\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democrat nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety Here's how the process worked", "body": "Harris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety {"uri": "8261824427", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "12:49:45", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T12:49:45Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T12:49:01Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8039215803146362, "url": "https://pantagraph.com/harris-wins-democratic-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_12bd629d-4930-5c02-8804-ef960320ccc7.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "By ROBERT YOON - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nKamala Harris is now Democratic presidential nominee, will face off against Donald Trump this fall By SEUNG MIN KIM - Associated Press\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nPeople are also reading... Rosie's Pub owner leaves beloved downtown business after 21 years 'Love is love' at annual Pridefest in downtown Bloomington Bloomington Route 9 construction, closures expected until summer 2025 Bloomington families displaced by Salvation Army project to house homeless veterans 17-year-old McLean County teen charged as an adult for attempted murder What are Gov. J.B. Pritzker's odds of becoming Kamala Harris' running mate? Move-out deadline extended for Bloomington residents displaced by Salvation Army project Woman dead, residents displaced after Eureka apartment fire Bloomington man gets 80 years for 2022 murder inside an apartment Flick: Suddenly, Normal seen all over the world Flick Fact: Are you in B-N shopping at Illinois' most visited department store? REO Speedwagon, Loverboy to perform in Bloomington this fall El Paso's Michael Mounce joins elite Bloomington-Normal Golf Association company Chicago man gets 103 years for 2020 shooting death of a Bloomington man Funerals pending for Aug. 1\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8261818378", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "12:46:03", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T12:46:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T12:45:08Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8039215803146362, "url": "https://mcdowellnews.com/harris-wins-democratic-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_5000c7a1-e27d-5cfe-bd74-fabbebbd8b73.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "WASHINGTON -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nKamala Harris is now Democratic presidential nominee, will face off against Donald Trump this fall By SEUNG MIN KIM - Associated Press\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nPeople are also reading... Sheetz gas station, convenience store planned for Marion Sheriff: Man wanted for break-in, assault at McDowell County home Marion police offering free food, fun at National Night Out McDowell County man killed after jumping from pickup truck in Glen Alpine identified Marion's Sheetz gas station expected to open by early 2025 West Marion speaker on McDowell's strategic planning: No Black or Brown people on committee Roses Discount Store in Marion set to close in August McDowell Tech president talks food, pets and church in meeting with local officials McDowell's hospital recognized by Newsweek and Fortune magazines Public meeting on 1,300-acre Burke, McDowell business site set for Aug. 6 Catawba County woman sentenced for stealing over $300,000 from elderly relatives Biscuitville named top fast-food chain for breakfast - again Stony Point woman charged with stealing $16,000 from Taylorsville church Officials: Morganton hospital's 6-story patient tower will triple size of emergency department Sheriff: Marion man intentionally hit pedestrian with car\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. California awards Le Petit Elephant $1.5 million to convert Napa church to day care center Napa Police: Store theft leads to pursuit, crash, possible charges St. Helenans cycle from San Francisco to Los Angeles to raise awareness of AIDS 'No place in our community,' police and DA say after antisemitic flyers scattered in Napa Chevron says it is moving its headquarters out of California Letter: A mom's thoughts about Le Petit Elephant Dan Berger On Wine: Hard times in the industry CHP: Electric unicyclist suffers major injuries in Carneros hit-and-run Register Explainer: A deeper look at Belia Ramos vs. Debra Dommen Napa Town & Country Fair returns on Thursday\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8261207727", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "06:05:15", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T06:05:15Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T06:04:34Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7960784435272217, "url": "https://www.bostonglobe.com/2024/08/06/nation/harris-wins-democratic-presidential-nomination-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-process-worked/", "title": "Harris wins Democratic presidential nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked. - The Boston Globe", "body": "Harris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Answer with FRANCE 24's international affairs commentator Dougl...\n\nWASHINGTON -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nKamala Harris is now Democratic presidential nominee, will face off against Donald Trump this fall\n\nBy SEUNG MIN KIM - Associated Press\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nWaterloo residents have more than 1,000 signatures on petition to put high school plan on the ballot Timeline: Deere has cut 2,700 jobs in 10 months in Iowa and Illinois Helicopter crashed near Nashua after striking powerlines, killing pilot UPDATE: Man charged with TC damage said he was 'blackout drunk' West Lake Okoboji home sells for state record $9.5M Bremer Avenue bridge in Waverly to be demolished, closed for a year starting Thursday Autistic man accused of breaking into co-worker's home to plant letter, smear boyfriend Pablo's Mexican Grill marking 20 years on Main Street in downtown Cedar Falls UAW releases statement on John Deere layoffs, says cuts 'kill good American jobs' Man sentenced to prison in shooting that paralyzed robbery victim Lawsuit by man paralyzed in Waterloo officer-involved shooting thrown out 2024 Courier Male Athlete of the Year: Jesup's Jack Miller 'It helped me a lot': Suni Lee has 'needed' heart-to-heart with teammates before Team USA won gold Cedar Falls City Council to consider items related to more planned roundabouts STEVE CORBIN: Economists say Trump poses a threat to US economy\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press.
