{"uri": "2024-08-445232042", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "04:56:52", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T04:56:52Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T04:36:57Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7803921699523926, "url": "https://wcfcourier.com/partners/ap/national/harris-wins-democratic-presidential-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_3cd06ac3-4412-57c7-ad0b-f95cb3614c40.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic presidential nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "FILE - Vice President Kamala Harris arrives to speak during a campaign rally, July 30, 2024, in Atlanta. Harris, the daughter of immigrants who rose through the California political and law enforcement ranks to become the first female vice president in U.S. history, is poised to secure the Democratic Party's presidential nomination Monday, Aug. 5, 2024.\n\nBy ROBERT YOON - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nTimeline: Deere has cut 2,700 jobs in 10 months in Iowa and Illinois Waterloo residents have more than 1,000 signatures on petition to put high school plan on the ballot West Lake Okoboji home sells for state record $9.5M Bremer Avenue bridge in Waverly to be demolished, closed for a year starting Thursday Helicopter crashed near Nashua after striking powerlines, killing pilot UPDATE: Man charged with TC damage said he was 'blackout drunk' Autistic man accused of breaking into co-worker's home to plant letter, smear boyfriend Pablo's Mexican Grill marking 20 years on Main Street in downtown Cedar Falls Man charged with breaking into home to steal wiring for scrap UAW releases statement on John Deere layoffs, says cuts 'kill good American jobs' Waterloo school board approves $165 million plan to to merge high schools by 2028-29 Allen Hospital among nation's top performing hospitals for treatment of heart attack 2024 Courier Male Athlete of the Year: Jesup's Jack Miller 'It helped me a lot': Suni Lee has 'needed' heart-to-heart with teammates before Team USA won gold Waterloo man arrested following short chase\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8261816670", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "12:45:21", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T12:45:21Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T12:44:04Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7764706015586853, "url": "https://www.stltoday.com/harris-wins-democratic-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_12f82e47-a16f-5519-83cb-2396c6943897.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "By ROBERT YOON - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nInterim superintendent of St. Louis Public Schools left previous job under fire Vatican rejects appeals to save two Catholic parishes in St. Louis A beloved South Grand convenience store employee was killed by customer, witnesses say Michael Siani's injury will throw open range of options in center field: Cardinals Extra Videos show CVPA shooter stalking hallways before police storm the St. Louis school McClellan: Maybe Outsiders aren't the answer for St. Louis Man claims to be editor of St. Louis' Riverfront Times, defends site's OnlyFans content Transgender woman's use of locker room at Ellisville gym sparks outcry from conservatives Firm with ties to Indian tech execs made $1.5 million donation to Missouri treasurer BenFred: Hey, maybe we weren't crazy for thinking Caitlin Clark should have made Team USA Editorial: Our endorsements in Missouri's GOP and Democratic gubernatorial primaries St. Louis restaurant openings and closings for July St. Louis County overpaid dozens of employees for years. Some had already quit. Sultan: What J.D. Vance's obsession with childless cat women is really about Target plans another location in St. Louis County\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. {"uri": "8262010945", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "14:51:28", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T14:51:28Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T14:50:31Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.772549033164978, "url": "https://journalstar.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/harris-wins-democratic-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_8778aa10-d442-5abf-854a-9952852d2419.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "WASHINGTON -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nGovernment-politics Kamala Harris is now Democratic presidential nominee, will face off against Donald Trump this fall By SEUNG MIN KIM - Associated Press\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nPeople are also reading... Lincoln cookie shop closes as owner loses possession of downtown building Demolition of north Lincoln grain elevator could begin as early as October Lincoln works to clean up trees, restore power after storms; governor declares emergency Nebraska education board passes statewide dress code policy to protect student beliefs Pedestrian hit, killed near Lincoln casino Teddy Prochazka is 'playing free' as he finally enters training camp at full strength Former small-town Nebraska bank president sentenced to federal prison for bank fraud Take an inside look at the soon-to-open WarHorse Casino in Omaha Lincoln police identify man who died in Sunday morning crash near casino Nebraska lawmaker's proposed Taxpayer's Bill of Rights draws opponents from Colorado Who is Italian boxer Angela Carini and why did she quit her fight against Imane Khelif? Severe thunderstorm hits Lincoln area; tens of thousands without power New Lincoln high schools continue to see rapid growth Cover Five: Nebraska's position battles at offensive line, cornerback, wide receiver Storm knocks out power to Lincoln's wellfields near Ashland\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. Review: T-Pain deserved better than what fans gave him at the Mississippi Valley Fair Timeline: Deere has cut 2,700 jobs in 10 months in Iowa and Illinois, 1,300 in Quad-Cities UAW releases statement on John Deere layoffs, says cuts 'kill good American jobs' Bettendorf police arrest man on suspicion of human trafficking, pimping Review: The bests and worsts of Friday at Hinterland Music Festival Davenport man arrested in connection with robbery, shooting incident Man accused of stealing car at gunpoint in Davenport Editorial: Davenport can change trajectory with new city administrator Mount Mercy and St. Ambrose to combine into single university Gannon Hanevold: The three strangest foods we found at the Mississippi Valley Fair John Deere lays off more than 300 in Moline and East Moline in latest round of cuts Parents of murder victim angry with plea deal that offered reduced charge and prison sentence Five named to St. Ambrose Hall of Fame Driver of garbage truck struck by train in Scott County suffers minor injuries Prosecutors ask to dismiss cases against two men for 2023 killing in Davenport\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8261129138", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "04:55:04", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T04:55:04Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T04:54:18Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7647058963775635, "url": "https://mcdowellnews.com/partners/ap/national/harris-wins-democratic-presidential-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_4bb0b7b1-855b-58aa-9fef-3b0febf59ccd.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic presidential nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "By ROBERT YOON - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nPeople are also reading... Sheetz gas station, convenience store planned for Marion Sheriff: Man wanted for break-in, assault at McDowell County home Marion police offering free food, fun at National Night Out McDowell County man killed after jumping from pickup truck in Glen Alpine identified Marion's Sheetz gas station expected to open by early 2025 West Marion speaker on McDowell's strategic planning: No Black or Brown people on committee Roses Discount Store in Marion set to close in August McDowell Tech president talks food, pets and church in meeting with local officials Public meeting on 1,300-acre Burke, McDowell business site set for Aug. 6 Catawba County woman sentenced for stealing over $300,000 from elderly relatives McDowell's hospital recognized by Newsweek and Fortune magazines Biscuitville named top fast-food chain for breakfast - again Stony Point woman charged with stealing $16,000 from Taylorsville church Officials: Morganton hospital's 6-story patient tower will triple size of emergency department Sheriff: Marion man intentionally hit pedestrian with car\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8261995089", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "14:40:45", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T14:40:45Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T14:39:51Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7647058963775635, "url": "https://napavalleyregister.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/harris-wins-democratic-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_9fc90f05-dc80-5e67-b426-9e57a1af66f4.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "WASHINGTON -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nGovernment-politics Kamala Harris is now Democratic presidential nominee, will face off against Donald Trump this fall By SEUNG MIN KIM - Associated Press\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nPeople are also reading... Woman dies after being found near Highway 29 in St. Helena Napa Main Street downtown block home to Cole's, Ben & Jerry's, others sold for $17.5 million Update: Pedestrian, Calistoga cop involved in crash identified; woman critically injured Wine attorney Richard Mendelson says Napa Valley can find 'common ground' Napa's Soscol Junction makes its partial debut. A Register reporter tests it. Napa Police: Store theft leads to pursuit, crash, possible charges California awards Le Petit Elephant $1.5 million to convert Napa church to day care center St. Helenans cycle from San Francisco to Los Angeles to raise awareness of AIDS 'No place in our community,' police and DA say after antisemitic flyers scattered in Napa Chevron says it is moving its headquarters out of California Letter: A mom's thoughts about Le Petit Elephant Dan Berger On Wine: Hard times in the industry CHP: Electric unicyclist suffers major injuries in Carneros hit-and-run Update: Napa Police ends South Jefferson Street shelter-in-place order Register Explainer: A deeper look at Belia Ramos vs. Debra Dommen\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. DNC rules allowed any candidate to compete for the nomination if they received the electronic signatures of at least 300 of the more than 4,000 delegates, but Harris was the only one who received it.\n\nShe quickly consolidated support after throwing her hat in the ring for the nomination soon after Biden stepped aside, with enough a majority of delegates publicly declaring their support for her by the end of the next day after she launched her candidacy. {"uri": "2024-08-445755297", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "13:19:35", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T13:19:35Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T12:30:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7568627595901489, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/harris-wins-democratic-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_4d4b1c33-1ad1-5987-8f0a-bff3e193394d.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "Kamala Harris was set to announce her running mate Tuesday before the pair head out on a five-day swing through America's hottest battleground states, just three months out from the presidential election. Who are the contenders? Answer with FRANCE 24's international affairs commentator Dougl...\n\nWASHINGTON -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nKamala Harris is now Democratic presidential nominee, will face off against Donald Trump this fall\n\nBy SEUNG MIN KIM - Associated Press\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nTom and Molly Bedell's West Lake Okoboji home sells for state record $9.5M 'We'll miss the place': Home on Sioux City's north side on market for first time in decades A shellfish guest: Chesapeake blue crab found at Sioux City Library Sierra signage going up at former Sioux City Bed Bath & Beyond Sioux City's original guitar hero to be celebrated with a three-night musical extravaganza Olympic Athletics Results: USD's Chris Nilsen finishes 11th in pole vault group Police release name of man pulled from Floyd River Toast on Hill offering classy breakfast and lunch to Okoboji Lakes visitors A favorite Sioux City deli stages a comeback with a new lobby and full menu of sandwiches Simon Max Kaplan Fireworks serve as weekly grand finale for Arnolds Park's Saturday night concert series Sioux City schools vandalized with graffiti over weekend Sioux City Council to vote on resolution for faith-based treatment center City of Sioux City issues guidance for tree limb disposal Marriage licenses for August 5, 2024\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. {"uri": "8261812580", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-06", "time": "12:42:38", "dateTime": "2024-08-06T12:42:38Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-06T12:41:20Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7411764860153198, "url": "https://lacrossetribune.com/harris-wins-democratic-nomination-in-virtual-roll-call-heres-how-the-process-worked/article_c535e58d-c0e9-50c2-8f4c-6ec6b72d54d4.html", "title": "Harris wins Democratic nomination in virtual roll call. Here's how the process worked", "body": "WASHINGTON -- Democratic delegates have selected Vice President Kamala Harris to be the party's presidential nominee, according to final vote results released late Monday by the Democratic National Committee. But don't expect a balloon drop just yet.\n\nHarris officially claimed the nomination following a five-day online voting process, receiving nearly 4,600 votes, which it said was 99% of participating delegates. The party did not release a precise number of votes that were cast for \"present\" or the state-by-state breakdown of \"present\" votes, though it did release state-by-state numbers for Harris.\n\nKamala Harris is now Democratic presidential nominee, will face off against Donald Trump this fall By SEUNG MIN KIM - Associated Press\n\nHarris had already secured the majority of votes needed to claim the nomination by Friday afternoon, DNC Chair Jaime Harrison announced in a live video presentation that day to mark the milestone, but the result was not official until after the voting period closed at 6 p.m. ET on Monday.\n\nPeople are also reading... Valley View Mall in La Crosse closed for third consecutive day following power outage Onalaska man faces felony charge after injury hit-and-run in La Crosse Finnottes Candies owner marks end of 37-year business with final sales and memories Steve Cahalan: Red Lobster building to be razed to make way for car wash Steve Cahalan: Blair Meat Market expands, Red Lobster restaurant to be razed for new car wash 'The truck is their house': Onalaska trucking instructor offers free CDL classes for homeless jobseekers New public hearing date set to discuss overnight unsheltered sleeping areas in La Crosse Big Lots closing La Crosse store; chain may be headed toward bankruptcy VP Kamala Harris to visit western Wisconsin with running mate next week Defense attorney raises insanity plea for man accused of killing his wife in their town of Shelby home Homeless and dying: La Crosse veteran diagnosed with heart failure finds peace in final weeks La Crosse man faces charges for driving under the influence of marijuana Child neglect charges filed against La Crosse couple after baby's fentanyl death La Crosse man charged with felony drug possession after cocaine seized during traffic stop 2 injured in Trempealeau County accident\n\nDelegates to the Democratic National Convention began casting votes on Thursday to select their nominee for president, but unlike in past years, they did so not in the raucous party atmosphere of the convention floor or even during the convention itself. Instead, they participated in what the party called a \"virtual roll call,\" filling out electronic ballots from their homes, offices or vacation spots more than two weeks before the first delegate steps foot inside Chicago's United Center.\n\nHarris was the only candidate eligible to receive votes after no other candidate qualified by a Tuesday night deadline.\n\nHow does the virtual process work?\n\nDelegates used an electronic voting method the party says is similar to one used to tally virtual roll call votes in the 2020 convention, when the COVID-19 pandemic forced the party to conduct much of its official business remotely.\n\nUnder new procedures adopted by the convention's rules committee in late July, candidates had until Tuesday to declare their intent to seek the nomination and until that night to electronically submit the 300 delegate signatures required to qualify for the roll call vote. According to a DNC statement, Harris submitted signatures from 3,923 delegates, about 84% of the full delegation and 99% of delegates who signed a petition.\n\nUnder party rules, where only officially nominated candidates were eligible to receive votes, votes cast for someone other than Harris in the roll call were counted as \"present.\"\n\nWho gets to vote?\n\nAmong those eligible to vote were close to 4,000 pledged delegates selected through state primary and caucus processes as well as more than 700 others who have automatic delegate slots by virtue of the elected office or party positions they hold. These include Democratic governors, U.S. senators and representatives, former presidents and DNC members.\n\nThese automatic delegates, known informally as superdelegates, were the subject of much debate within the party for years because of their potential to influence the outcome of a close nomination fight despite not having been selected for their posts through a public process. Historically, however, superdelegates have never backed a candidate for the nomination other than the one who also earned the majority of pledged delegates.\n\nAfter the 2016 election, the party passed changes that allowed superdelegates to vote only on the second and subsequent ballots. Party rules do allow superdelegates to vote on the first ballot if a candidate has either won a majority of pledged delegates through the primary and caucus process, like President Joe Biden did, or submitted the signatures of a majority of total delegates, as Harris did.\n\nWhat about the nominee for vice president?\n\nNow that Harris is the Democratic presidential nominee, the new convention rules allow her to place the name of her pick for vice president into nomination, at which point the convention chair can declare that candidate as the vice presidential nominee.\n\nHarris is closing in on selecting a running mate and spent the weekend interviewing possible candidates. She and whomever she selects will head out on a seven-state swing of key battlegrounds, including Pennsylvania, Arizona and North Carolina, this week.\n\nWhy did this happen before the convention?\n\nDNC officials first indicated in May that they would conduct a virtual roll call to clear a potential hurdle in getting the Democratic nominee on the ballot in Ohio. Ohio's deadline to file for the general election ballot is Wednesday, two weeks before Democratic delegates would have crowned the nominee at the convention.\n\nAlthough the deadline had been modified in previous presidential election years to accommodate late-summer conventions of both parties, this year state Republicans initially planned to enforce the existing deadline, with one GOP lawmaker calling the scheduling bind \" a Democratic problem.\"\n\nThe Republican-controlled Legislature did eventually make the change at the behest of Republican Gov. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nPhotos: Kamala Harris through the years\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. Mike DeWine, but the law does not go into effect until Aug. 31. Citing concerns that Ohio Republicans could still try to block their candidate from getting on the ballot despite the legislative fix, DNC officials moved forward with their virtual roll call as originally planned.\n\nSo what happens at the convention?\n\nThe main order of business at any national party convention is officially selecting nominees for president and vice president and for the nominees to deliver speeches accepting their nominations.\n\nTypically, the process of voting for the nominee is done publicly, with a live roll call vote in which state delegations cast their votes from the convention floor with much fanfare. This year's pre-convention vote for the Democratic nominee was conducted behind closed doors, with results released only at the conclusion of the vote, instead of the real-time vote updates that have been a fixture of past convention roll call votes.\n\nStill, in this year's virtual roll call, as with in-person roll calls going back decades, the outcome of the vote was essentially known long before the first vote was cast. Despite the period of uncertainty immediately following Biden's withdrawal from the race, an AP survey indicated that delegates overwhelmingly coalesced behind Harris to replace Biden on the ticket, leaving little suspense heading into the online vote.\n\nThe DNC says the convention will feature a ceremonial roll call vote, mimicking the look of a traditional convention roll call. Harris will still deliver an acceptance speech on the last day of the convention.\n\nIn addition to selecting presidential and vice presidential nominees, convention delegates participate in a variety of other business, including adopting the party's platform.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press.
